<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Concussion</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Concussion_(disambiguation)" title="Concussion (disambiguation)">Concussion (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em">
<tr>
<th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold;background:#ccc">Concussion</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Synonyms</th>
<td>Mild brain injury, mild traumatic brain injury (MTBI), mild head injury (MHI), minor head trauma</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Concussion_mechanics.svg"><img alt="Concussion mechanics.svg" data-file-height="633" data-file-width="538" height="353" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Concussion_mechanics.svg/300px-Concussion_mechanics.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Concussion_mechanics.svg/450px-Concussion_mechanics.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Concussion_mechanics.svg/600px-Concussion_mechanics.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/Acceleration" title="Acceleration">Acceleration</a> (g-forces) can exert rotational forces in the brain, especially the <a href="/wiki/Midbrain" title="Midbrain">midbrain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diencephalon" title="Diencephalon">diencephalon</a>.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Specialty_(medicine)" title="Specialty (medicine)">Specialty</a></th>
<td><a href="/wiki/Neurology" title="Neurology">Neurology</a></td>
</tr>
<tr class="noprint">
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<div style="text-align: right;">[<a class="extiw" href="https://www.wikidata.org/wiki/Q326921" title="d:Q326921">edit on Wikidata</a>]</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Concussion</b>, also known as <b>minor head trauma</b> or <b>mild traumatic brain injury (mTBI)</b> is the most common type of <a href="/wiki/Traumatic_brain_injury" title="Traumatic brain injury">traumatic brain injury</a>. It is typically defined as a <a href="/wiki/Head_injury" title="Head injury">head injury</a> with a temporary loss of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brain_function" title="Brain function">brain function</a>. Symptoms include a variety of physical, <a href="/wiki/Cognition" title="Cognition">cognitive</a>, and emotional symptoms, which may not be recognized if subtle. A variety of signs accompany concussion including headache, feeling in a fog, and emotional changeability. In general, the signs can be categorized into physical signs (such as loss of consciousness or amnesia), behavioral changes (such as irritability), cognitive impairment (such as slowed reaction times), and sleep disturbances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2008_Consensus_Statement_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-2008_Consensus_Statement-1">[1]</a></sup> Fewer than 10% of sports-related concussions among children are associated with loss of consciousness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pediatrics.2C_Sports_Related_Concussion_in_Children_and_Adolescents.2C_2010_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pediatrics.2C_Sports_Related_Concussion_in_Children_and_Adolescents.2C_2010-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Common causes include <a href="/wiki/Concussions_in_sport" title="Concussions in sport">sports injuries</a>, bicycle accidents, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Car_accidents" title="Car accidents">car accidents</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Falling_(accident)" title="Falling (accident)">falls</a>, the latter two being the most frequent causes among adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17215534_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17215534-3">[3]</a></sup> In addition to a blow to the head, concussion may be caused by <a href="/wiki/Acceleration" title="Acceleration">acceleration</a> forces without a direct impact, and on the battlefield, MTBI is a potential consequence of nearby explosions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> It is not clear exactly what damage is done and how the symptoms are caused, but stretching of <a href="/wiki/Axon" title="Axon">axons</a> and changes in <a href="/wiki/Ion_channel" title="Ion channel">ion channels</a> are involved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shaw02_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shaw02-6">[6]</a></sup> Cellular damage has reportedly been found in concussed brains, but it may have been due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-ray_computed_tomography#Artifacts" title="X-ray computed tomography">artifacts</a> from the studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rees03_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rees03-7">[7]</a></sup> It is currently thought that structural and <a href="/wiki/Neuropsychiatry" title="Neuropsychiatry">neuropsychiatric</a> factors may both be responsible for the effects of concussion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Treatment involves monitoring as well as physical and cognitive rest (reduction of such activities as school work, playing video games and text messaging).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2008_Consensus_Statement_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-2008_Consensus_Statement-1">[1]</a></sup> Symptoms usually resolve within three weeks, though they may persist or complications may occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AndersonT_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-AndersonT-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>The rate at which concussion occurs is not accurately known, but is estimated to be more than 6 per 1,000 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CassidyWHO_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-CassidyWHO-10">[10]</a></sup> Those who have had one concussion seem more susceptible to another, especially if the new injury occurs before symptoms from the previous concussion have completely resolved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merck_Home_Health_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Merck_Home_Health-11">[11]</a></sup> There is also a negative progressive process in which smaller impacts cause the same symptom severity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2008_Consensus_Statement_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-2008_Consensus_Statement-1">[1]</a></sup> Repeated concussions may increase the risk in later life for dementia, Parkinson's disease, or depression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merck_Home_Health_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Merck_Home_Health-11">[11]</a></sup></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Signs_and_symptoms">Signs and symptoms</span></h2>
<p>Concussion is associated with a variety of symptoms, which typically occur rapidly after the injury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aubry_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-aubry-12">[12]</a></sup> Early symptoms usually subside within days or weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rees03_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rees03-7">[7]</a></sup> The number and type of symptoms any one individual suffers varies widely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ComperBisschop_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-ComperBisschop-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical">Physical</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Headache" title="Headache">Headache</a> is the most common MTBI symptom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kushner98_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kushner98-14">[14]</a></sup> Others include dizziness, vomiting, nausea, lack of <a href="/wiki/Motor_coordination" title="Motor coordination">motor coordination</a>, difficulty <a href="/wiki/Balance_(ability)" title="Balance (ability)">balancing</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kushner98_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kushner98-14">[14]</a></sup> or other problems with movement or sensation. Visual symptoms include <a href="/wiki/Photophobia" title="Photophobia">light sensitivity</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowen03_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bowen03-15">[15]</a></sup> seeing bright lights,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cantu01_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-cantu01-16">[16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Blurred_vision" title="Blurred vision">blurred vision</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rees03_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Rees03-7">[7]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Diplopia" title="Diplopia">double vision</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erlanger99_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Erlanger99-17">[17]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tinnitus" title="Tinnitus">Tinnitus</a>, or a ringing in the ears, is also commonly reported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rees03_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-Rees03-7">[7]</a></sup> In one in about seventy concussions, concussive <a href="/wiki/Convulsion" title="Convulsion">convulsions</a> occur, but seizures that take place during or immediately after concussion are not "<a href="/wiki/Post-traumatic_seizure" title="Post-traumatic seizure">post-traumatic seizures</a>", and, unlike post-traumatic seizures, are not predictive of <a href="/wiki/Post-traumatic_epilepsy" title="Post-traumatic epilepsy">post-traumatic epilepsy</a>, which requires some form of structural brain damage, not just a momentary disruption in normal brain functioning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCrory1998_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-McCrory1998-18">[18]</a></sup> Concussive convulsions are thought to result from temporary loss or inhibition of motor function, and are not associated either with epilepsy or with more serious structural damage. They are not associated with any particular <a href="/wiki/Sequela" title="Sequela">sequelae</a>, and have the same high rate of favorable outcomes as concussions without convulsions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perron_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Perron-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cognitive_and_emotional">Cognitive and emotional</span></h3>
<p>Cognitive symptoms include confusion, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disorientation" title="Disorientation">disorientation</a>, and difficulty focusing <a href="/wiki/Attention" title="Attention">attention</a>. Loss of consciousness may occur, but is not necessarily correlated with the severity of the concussion if it is brief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-canturc06_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-canturc06-20">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Post-traumatic_amnesia" title="Post-traumatic amnesia">Post-traumatic amnesia</a>, in which events following the injury cannot be recalled, is a hallmark of concussion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kushner98_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kushner98-14">[14]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mental_confusion" title="Mental confusion">Confusion</a>, another concussion hallmark, may be present immediately or may develop over several minutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kushner98_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-Kushner98-14">[14]</a></sup> A person may repeat the same questions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aanMgmt_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-aanMgmt-21">[21]</a></sup> be slow to respond to questions or directions, have a vacant stare, or have slurred<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kushner98_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-Kushner98-14">[14]</a></sup> or incoherent speech.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-foundations_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-foundations-22">[22]</a></sup> Other MTBI symptoms include changes in sleeping patterns<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rees03_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-Rees03-7">[7]</a></sup> and difficulty with reasoning,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erlanger99_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Erlanger99-17">[17]</a></sup> concentrating, and performing everyday activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kushner98_14-5"><a href="#cite_note-Kushner98-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>Concussion can result in changes in <a href="/wiki/Affect_(psychology)" title="Affect (psychology)">mood</a> including crankiness, loss of interest in favorite activities or items,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mayoc_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-mayoc-23">[23]</a></sup> tearfulness,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivak_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-sivak-24">[24]</a></sup> and displays of emotion that are inappropriate to the situation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-foundations_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-foundations-22">[22]</a></sup> Common symptoms in concussed children include restlessness, lethargy, and irritability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heegaard07_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heegaard07-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanism">Mechanism</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gatica1.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1150" data-file-width="1628" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Gatica1.png/220px-Gatica1.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Gatica1.png/330px-Gatica1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Gatica1.png/440px-Gatica1.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gatica1.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Rotational force is key in concussion. Punches in <a href="/wiki/Boxing" title="Boxing">boxing</a> can deliver more rotational force to the head than the typical impact in <a href="/wiki/American_football" title="American football">American football</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pellman_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pellman-26">[26]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Human_brain" title="Human brain">brain</a> is surrounded by <a href="/wiki/Cerebrospinal_fluid" title="Cerebrospinal fluid">cerebrospinal fluid</a>, which protects it from light trauma. More severe impacts, or the forces associated with rapid acceleration, may not be absorbed by this cushion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shaw02_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shaw02-6">[6]</a></sup> Concussion may be caused by impact <a href="/wiki/Force" title="Force">forces</a>, in which the head strikes or is struck by something, or impulsive forces, in which the head moves without itself being subject to <a href="/wiki/Blunt_trauma" title="Blunt trauma">blunt trauma</a> (for example, when the chest hits something and the head snaps forward).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivak_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-sivak-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>Forces may cause linear, rotational, or angular movement of the brain, or a combination of them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivak_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-sivak-24">[24]</a></sup> In rotational movement, the head turns around its center of gravity, and in angular movement it turns on an axis not through its center of gravity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivak_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-sivak-24">[24]</a></sup> The amount of rotational force is thought to be the major component in concussion<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poirier_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Poirier-27">[27]</a></sup> and its severity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AndersonT_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-AndersonT-9">[9]</a></sup> Studies with athletes have shown that the amount of force and the location of the impact are not necessarily correlated with the severity of the concussion or its symptoms, and have called into question the threshold for concussion previously thought to exist at around 70–75 <a href="/wiki/G-force" title="G-force">g</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guskiewicz_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guskiewicz-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gever07_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gever07-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>The parts of the brain most affected by rotational forces are the <a href="/wiki/Midbrain" title="Midbrain">midbrain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diencephalon" title="Diencephalon">diencephalon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pearce_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pearce-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17215534_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17215534-3">[3]</a></sup> It is thought that the forces from the injury disrupt the normal <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cellular</a> activities in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reticular_activating_system" title="Reticular activating system">reticular activating system</a> located in these areas, and that this disruption produces the loss of consciousness often seen in concussion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17215534_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17215534-3">[3]</a></sup> Other areas of the brain that may be affected include the upper part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brain_stem" title="Brain stem">brain stem</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fornix_of_brain" title="Fornix of brain">fornix</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Corpus_callosum" title="Corpus callosum">corpus callosum</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Temporal_lobe" title="Temporal lobe">temporal lobe</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Frontal_lobe" title="Frontal lobe">frontal lobe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biglered_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-biglered-31">[31]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Angular_acceleration" title="Angular acceleration">Angular accelerations</a> of 4600, 5900, or 7900 <a href="/wiki/Radian" title="Radian">radian</a>/s<sup>2</sup> are estimated to have 25, 50, or 80% risk of MTBI respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pathophysiology">Pathophysiology</span></h3>
<p>In both animals and humans, MTBI can alter the brain's physiology for hours to years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mouzon2014_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mouzon2014-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith2013_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith2013-34">[34]</a></sup> setting into motion a variety of <a href="/wiki/Pathology" title="Pathology">pathological</a> events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11910544_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11910544-35">[35]</a></sup> As one example, in animal models, after an initial increase in glucose metabolism, there is a subsequent reduced metabolic state which may persist for up to four weeks after injury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pediatrics.2C_Sports_Related_Concussion_in_Children_and_Adolescents.2C_2010_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pediatrics.2C_Sports_Related_Concussion_in_Children_and_Adolescents.2C_2010-2">[2]</a></sup> Though these events are thought to interfere with neuronal and brain function, the <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolic</a> processes that follow concussion are reversible in a large majority of affected <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brain_cell" title="Brain cell">brain cells</a>; however, a few cells may die after the injury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iverson2005_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Iverson2005-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Included in the cascade of events unleashed in the brain by concussion is impaired <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmission" title="Neurotransmission">neurotransmission</a>, loss of regulation of <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a>, deregulation of energy use and cellular metabolism, and a reduction in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cerebral_blood_flow" title="Cerebral blood flow">cerebral blood flow</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iverson2005_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Iverson2005-36">[36]</a></sup> Excitatory <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">neurotransmitters</a>, chemicals such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glutamate" title="Glutamate">glutamate</a> that serve to stimulate nerve cells, are released in excessive amounts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Team_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Team-37">[37]</a></sup> The resulting cellular excitation causes <a href="/wiki/Action_potential" title="Action potential">neurons to fire</a> excessively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GizaHovda_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-GizaHovda-38">[38]</a></sup> This creates an imbalance of ions such as <a href="/wiki/Potassium_in_biology" title="Potassium in biology">potassium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calcium_in_biology" title="Calcium in biology">calcium</a> across the cell membranes of neurons (a process like <a href="/wiki/Excitotoxicity" title="Excitotoxicity">excitotoxicity</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iverson2005_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-Iverson2005-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the same time, cerebral blood flow is relatively reduced for unknown reasons,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowen03_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bowen03-15">[15]</a></sup> though the reduction in blood flow is not as severe as it is in <a href="/wiki/Ischemia" title="Ischemia">ischemia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iverson2005_36-3"><a href="#cite_note-Iverson2005-36">[36]</a></sup> Thus cells get less glucose than they normally do, which causes an "energy crisis".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowen03_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bowen03-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Concurrently with these processes, the activity of <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrion" title="Mitochondrion">mitochondria</a> may be reduced, which causes cells to rely on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaerobic_metabolism" title="Anaerobic metabolism">anaerobic metabolism</a> to produce energy, increasing levels of the byproduct <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid" title="Lactic acid">lactate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iverson2005_36-4"><a href="#cite_note-Iverson2005-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>For a period of minutes to days after a concussion, the brain is especially vulnerable to changes in <a href="/wiki/Intracranial_pressure" title="Intracranial pressure">intracranial pressure</a>, blood flow, and <a href="/wiki/Hypoxia_(medical)" title="Hypoxia (medical)">anoxia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowen03_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bowen03-15">[15]</a></sup> According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animal_study" title="Animal study">studies performed on animals</a> (which are not always applicable to humans), large numbers of neurons can die during this period in response to slight, normally innocuous changes in blood flow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowen03_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-Bowen03-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Concussion involves <a href="/wiki/Focal_and_diffuse_brain_injury" title="Focal and diffuse brain injury">diffuse (as opposed to focal) brain injury</a>, meaning that the dysfunction occurs over a widespread area of the brain rather than in a particular spot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hardman02_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hardman02-39">[39]</a></sup> It is thought to be a milder type of <a href="/wiki/Diffuse_axonal_injury" title="Diffuse axonal injury">diffuse axonal injury</a>, because <a href="/wiki/Axon" title="Axon">axons</a> may be injured to a minor extent due to stretching.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivak_24-4"><a href="#cite_note-sivak-24">[24]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animal_experimentation" title="Animal experimentation">Animal studies</a> in which primates were concussed have revealed damage to brain tissues such as small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petechial_hemorrhage" title="Petechial hemorrhage">petechial hemorrhages</a> and axonal injury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hall05_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hall05-40">[40]</a></sup> Axonal damage has been found in the brains of concussion sufferers who died from other causes, but inadequate blood flow to the brain due to other injuries may have contributed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rees03_7-5"><a href="#cite_note-Rees03-7">[7]</a></sup> Findings from a study of the brains of deceased NFL athletes who received concussions suggest that lasting damage is done by such injuries. This damage, the severity of which increases with the cumulative number of concussions sustained, can lead to a variety of other health issues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnn_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnn-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>The debate over whether concussion is a functional or structural phenomenon is ongoing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCrory_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-McCrory-42">[42]</a></sup> Structural damage has been found in the mildly traumatically injured brains of animals, but it is not clear whether these findings would apply to humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pearce_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pearce-30">[30]</a></sup> Such changes in brain structure could be responsible for certain symptoms such as visual disturbances, but other sets of symptoms, especially those of a psychological nature, are more likely to be caused by reversible pathophysiological changes in cellular function that occur after concussion, such as alterations in neurons' biochemistry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AndersonT_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-AndersonT-9">[9]</a></sup> These reversible changes could also explain why dysfunction is frequently temporary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCrory_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-McCrory-42">[42]</a></sup> A task force of head injury experts called the Concussion In Sport Group met in 2001 and decided that "concussion may result in neuropathological changes but the acute clinical symptoms largely reflect a functional disturbance rather than structural injury."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aubry_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-aubry-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>In summary, and extrapolating from animal studies, the pathology of a concussion seems to start with the disruption of the cell membrane of nerve cells. This results in a migration of potassium from within the cell into the extracellular space with subsequent release of glutamate which potentiates further potassium shift, in turn resulting in depolarization and suppression of nerve activity. In an effort to restore ion balance, the sodium-potassium ion pumps increase activity, which results in excessive ATP (<a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">adenosine triphosphate</a>) consumption and glucose utilization. Lactate accumulates but, paradoxically, cerebral blood flow decreases, which leads to a proposed "energy crisis." After this increase in glucose metabolism, there is a subsequent lower metabolic state which may persist for up to 4 weeks after injury. A completely separate pathway involves a large amount of calcium accumulating in cells, which may impair oxidative metabolism and begin further biochemical pathways that result in cell death. Again, both of these main pathways have been established from animal studies and the extent to which they apply to humans is still somewhat unclear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pediatrics.2C_Sports_Related_Concussion_in_Children_and_Adolescents.2C_2010_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pediatrics.2C_Sports_Related_Concussion_in_Children_and_Adolescents.2C_2010-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diagnosis">Diagnosis</span></h2>
<table class="wikitable" style="float: right; margin-left:15px; text-align:center">

</tr>

<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seizure" title="Seizure">Seizure</a></td>

<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anizokoria.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="204" data-file-width="852" height="69" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Anizokoria.JPG/290px-Anizokoria.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Anizokoria.JPG/435px-Anizokoria.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Anizokoria.JPG/580px-Anizokoria.JPG 2x" width="290"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anizokoria.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Anisocoria" title="Anisocoria">Unequal pupil size</a> is a sign of a brain injury possibly more serious than concussion.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Head trauma recipients are initially assessed to exclude a more severe <a href="/wiki/Medical_emergency" title="Medical emergency">emergency</a> such as an intracranial hemorrhage. This includes the "ABCs" (airway, breathing, circulation) and stabilization of the cervical spine which is assumed to be injured in any athlete who is found to be unconscious after head or neck injury. Indications that screening for more serious injury is needed include worsening of symptoms such as headache, persistent vomiting,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CookSchweer_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-CookSchweer-44">[44]</a></sup> increasing disorientation or a deteriorating level of consciousness,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kayteasdale_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kayteasdale-45">[45]</a></sup> seizures, and <a href="/wiki/Anisocoria" title="Anisocoria">unequal pupil size</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cdcfacts_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-cdcfacts-46">[46]</a></sup> Those with such symptoms, or those who are at higher risk for a more serious brain injury, may undergo <a href="/wiki/CT_scan" title="CT scan">brain imaging</a> to detect lesions and are frequently observed for 24–48 hours. A brain CT or brain MRI should be avoided unless there are progressive neurological symptoms, focal neurological findings or concern of skull fracture on exam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMSSMfive_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-AMSSMfive-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Diagnosis of MTBI is based on physical and neurological examination findings, duration of unconsciousness (usually less than 30 minutes) and post-traumatic amnesia (PTA; usually less than 24 hours), and the <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Coma_Scale" title="Glasgow Coma Scale">Glasgow Coma Scale</a> (MTBI sufferers have scores of 13 to 15).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BorgHolm04_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-BorgHolm04-48">[48]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Neuropsychological_test" title="Neuropsychological test">Neuropsychological tests</a> exist to measure cognitive function and the international consensus meeting in Zurich recommended the use of the SCAT2 test.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2008_Consensus_Statement_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-2008_Consensus_Statement-1">[1]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rees03_7-6"><a href="#cite_note-Rees03-7">[7]</a></sup> Such tests may be administered hours, days, or weeks after the injury, or at different times to demonstrate any trend.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moser_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-moser-49">[49]</a></sup> Increasingly, athletes are also being tested pre-season to provide a baseline for comparison in the event of an injury, though this may not reduce risk or affect return to play.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid10981754_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid10981754-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21228656_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21228656-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>If the <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Coma_Scale" title="Glasgow Coma Scale">Glasgow Coma Scale</a> is less than 15 at two hours, or less than 14 at any time, a CT is recommended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17215534_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17215534-3">[3]</a></sup> In addition, a CT scan is more likely to be performed if observation after discharge is not assured or <a href="/wiki/Substance_intoxication" title="Substance intoxication">intoxication</a> is present, there is suspected increased risk for bleeding, age greater than 60,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17215534_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17215534-3">[3]</a></sup> or less than 16. Most concussions, without complication, cannot be detected with MRI or CT scans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poirier_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Poirier-27">[27]</a></sup> However, changes have been reported on MRI and SPECT imaging in those with concussion and normal CT scans, and <a href="/wiki/Post-concussion_syndrome" title="Post-concussion syndrome">post-concussion syndrome</a> may be associated with abnormalities visible on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Single_photon_emission_computed_tomography" title="Single photon emission computed tomography">SPECT</a> and <a href="/wiki/Positron_emission_tomography" title="Positron emission tomography">PET scans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iverson2005_36-5"><a href="#cite_note-Iverson2005-36">[36]</a></sup> Mild head injury may or may not produce abnormal <a href="/wiki/Electroencephalography" title="Electroencephalography">EEG</a> readings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Binder86_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Binder86-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>Concussion may be under-diagnosed because of the lack of the highly noticeable signs and symptoms while athletes may minimize their injuries to remain in the competition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BarthVarney99_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-BarthVarney99-53">[53]</a></sup> A retrospective survey in 2005 suggested that more than 88% of concussions are unrecognized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Delaney_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Delaney-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>Diagnosis can be complex because concussion shares symptoms with other conditions. For example, post-concussion symptoms such as cognitive problems may be misattributed to brain injury when, in fact, due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post-traumatic_stress_disorder" title="Post-traumatic stress disorder">post-traumatic stress disorder</a> (PTSD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bryant08_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bryant08-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span></h3>
<p>No single definition of concussion, minor head injury,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SatzZaucha_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-SatzZaucha-56">[56]</a></sup> or mild traumatic brain injury is universally accepted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ComperBisschop_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-ComperBisschop-13">[13]</a></sup> In 2001, the expert Concussion in Sport Group of the first International Symposium on Concussion in Sport<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aubry_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-aubry-12">[12]</a></sup> defined concussion as "a complex pathophysiological process affecting the brain, induced by traumatic biomechanical forces."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-canturc06_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-canturc06-20">[20]</a></sup> It was agreed that concussion typically involves temporary impairment of neurological function that heals by itself within time, and that <a href="/wiki/Neuroimaging" title="Neuroimaging">neuroimaging</a> normally shows no gross structural changes to the brain as the result of the condition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AndersonT_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-AndersonT-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, although no structural brain damage occurs according to the classic definition,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> some researchers have included <a href="/wiki/Injury" title="Injury">injuries</a> in which structural damage has occurred and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Institute_for_Health_and_Clinical_Excellence" title="National Institute for Health and Clinical Excellence">National Institute for Health and Clinical Excellence</a> definition includes physiological or physical disruption in the brain's <a href="/wiki/Chemical_synapse" title="Chemical synapse">synapses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NICE_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-NICE-58">[58]</a></sup> Also, by definition, concussion has historically involved a loss of consciousness. However, the definition has evolved over time to include a change in consciousness, such as amnesia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuffGrant_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-RuffGrant-59">[59]</a></sup> although controversy continues about whether the definition should include only those injuries in which <a href="/wiki/Unconsciousness" title="Unconsciousness">loss of consciousness</a> occurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pearce_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pearce-30">[30]</a></sup> This debate resurfaces in some of the best-known concussion grading scales, in which those episodes involving loss of consciousness are graded as being more severe than those without.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cobb_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-cobb-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Definitions of mild traumatic brain injury (MTBI) were inconsistent until the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a>'s <a href="/wiki/ICD-10" title="ICD-10">International Statistical Classification of Diseases and Related Health Problems</a> (ICD-10) provided a consistent, authoritative definition across specialties in 1992.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Petchprapai07_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Petchprapai07-61">[61]</a></sup> Since then, various organizations such as the American Congress of Rehabilitation Medicine<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kushner98_14-6"><a href="#cite_note-Kushner98-14">[14]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/American_Psychiatric_Association" title="American Psychiatric Association">American Psychiatric Association</a> in its <i><a href="/wiki/Diagnostic_and_Statistical_Manual_of_Mental_Disorders" title="Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders">Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Petchprapai07_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Petchprapai07-61">[61]</a></sup> have defined MTBI using some combination of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loss_of_consciousness" title="Loss of consciousness">loss of consciousness</a> (LOC), <a href="/wiki/Post-traumatic_amnesia" title="Post-traumatic amnesia">post-traumatic amnesia</a> (PTA), and the <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Coma_Scale" title="Glasgow Coma Scale">Glasgow Coma Scale</a> (GCS).</p>
<p>Concussion falls under the classification of mild TBI,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeeLK07_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-LeeLK07-62">[62]</a></sup> but it is not clear whether concussion is implied in mild brain injury or mild head injury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LevinHS_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-LevinHS-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The date of the event predicted near this tag has passed. (December 2012)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup> "MTBI" and "concussion" are often treated as synonyms in medical literature<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kushner98_14-7"><a href="#cite_note-Kushner98-14">[14]</a></sup> but other injuries such as <a href="/wiki/Intracranial_hemorrhage" title="Intracranial hemorrhage">intracranial hemorrhages</a> (e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intra-axial_hematoma" title="Intra-axial hematoma">intra-axial hematoma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Epidural_hematoma" title="Epidural hematoma">epidural hematoma</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Subdural_hematoma" title="Subdural hematoma">subdural hematoma</a>) are not necessarily precluded in MTBI<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AndersonT_9-4"><a href="#cite_note-AndersonT-9">[9]</a></sup> or mild head injury,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vanderNaalt_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-vanderNaalt-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Savitsky00_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Savitsky00-65">[65]</a></sup> as they are in concussion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ParikhKoch_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-ParikhKoch-66">[66]</a></sup> MTBI associated with abnormal neuroimaging may be considered "complicated MTBI".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iverson2005_36-6"><a href="#cite_note-Iverson2005-36">[36]</a></sup> "Concussion" can be considered to imply a state in which brain function is temporarily impaired and "MTBI" to imply a <a href="/wiki/Pathophysiology" title="Pathophysiology">pathophysiological</a> state, but in practice few researchers and clinicians distinguish between the terms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AndersonT_9-5"><a href="#cite_note-AndersonT-9">[9]</a></sup> Descriptions of the condition, including the severity and the area of the brain affected, are now used more often than "concussion" in clinical neurology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LarnerAJ_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-LarnerAJ-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Grading_systems">Grading systems</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Concussion_grading_systems" title="Concussion grading systems">Concussion grading systems</a></div>
<p>At least 41 systems measure the severity, or grade, of a mild head injury,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AndersonT_9-6"><a href="#cite_note-AndersonT-9">[9]</a></sup> and there is little agreement about which is best.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cantu01_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-cantu01-16">[16]</a></sup> In an effort to simplify, the 2nd International Conference on Concussion in Sport, meeting in Prague in 2004, decided that these systems should be abandoned in favor of a 'simple' or 'complex' classification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> However, the 2008 meeting in Zurich abandoned the simple versus complex terminology, although the participants did agree to keep the concept that most (80–90%) concussions resolve in a short period (7–10 days), and although the recovery time frame may be longer in children and adolescents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2008_Consensus_Statement_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-2008_Consensus_Statement-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the past, the decision to allow athletes to return to participation was frequently based on the grade of concussion. However, current research and recommendations by professional organizations including the <a href="/wiki/National_Athletic_Trainers%27_Association" title="National Athletic Trainers' Association">National Athletic Trainers' Association</a> recommend against such use of these grading systems. Currently, injured athletes are prohibited from returning to play before they are symptom-free during both rest and exertion and until results of the <a href="/wiki/Neuropsychological_test" title="Neuropsychological test">neuropsychological tests</a> have returned to pre-injury levels.</p>
<p>Three grading systems have been most widely followed: by Robert Cantu, the <a href="/wiki/Colorado_Medical_Society" title="Colorado Medical Society">Colorado Medical Society</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Neurology" title="American Academy of Neurology">American Academy of Neurology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cobb_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-cobb-60">[60]</a></sup> Each employs three grades, as summarized in the following table:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cantu01_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-cantu01-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable" style="margin: 1em auto 1em auto; text-align:center">
<caption>Comparison of historic concussion grading scales – not currently recommended for use by medical professionals</caption>
<tr>
<th>Guidelines </th>
<th>Grade I</th>
<th>Grade II</th>
<th>Grade III</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<th>Cantu</th>
<td width="25%">Post-traumatic amnesia &lt;30 minutes, no loss of consciousness</td>
<td width="25%">Loss of consciousness &lt;5 minutes or amnesia lasting 30 minutes–24 hours</td>
<td width="25%">Loss of consciousness &gt;5 minutes or amnesia &gt;24 hours</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th>Colorado Medical Society</th>
<td>Confusion, no loss of consciousness</td>
<td>Confusion, post-traumatic amnesia, no loss of consciousness</td>
<td>Any loss of consciousness</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th>American Academy of Neurology</th>
<td>Confusion, symptoms last &lt;15 minutes, no loss of consciousness</td>
<td>Symptoms last &gt;15 minutes, no loss of consciousness</td>
<td>Loss of consciousness (IIIa, coma lasts seconds, IIIb for minutes)</td>
</tr>
</table>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention">Prevention</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Prevention_of_concussions" title="Prevention of concussions">Prevention of concussions</a></div>
<p>Prevention of MTBI involves general measures such as wearing <a href="/wiki/Seat_belt" title="Seat belt">seat belts</a> and using <a href="/wiki/Airbag" title="Airbag">airbags</a> in cars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kushner98_14-8"><a href="#cite_note-Kushner98-14">[14]</a></sup> Older people are encouraged to reduce fall risk by keeping floors free of clutter and wearing thin, flat, shoes with hard soles that do not interfere with balance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mayoc_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-mayoc-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Protective equipment such as headgear has been found to reduce the number of concussions in athletes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pellman_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pellman-26">[26]</a></sup> and improvements in the design of helmets may decrease the number and severity further.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LevyOzgur_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-LevyOzgur-69">[69]</a></sup> New "Head Impact Telemetry System" technology is being placed in helmets to study injury mechanisms and may generate knowledge that will potentially help reduce the risk of concussions among American Football players. Self-reported concussion rates among U-20 and elite rugby union players in Ireland are 45–48%. Half of these injuries go unreported. Changes to the rules or enforcing existing rules in sports, such as those against "head-down tackling", or "spearing", which is associated with a high injury rate, may also prevent concussions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pellman_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pellman-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Treatment">Treatment</span></h2>
<p>After exclusion of neck injury, observation should be continued for several hours. If repeated vomiting, worsening headache, dizziness, seizure activity, excessive drowsiness, double vision, slurred speech, unsteady walk, or weakness or numbness in arms or legs, or signs of <a href="/wiki/Basilar_skull_fracture" title="Basilar skull fracture">basilar skull fracture</a> develop, immediate assessment in an emergency department is warranted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2008_Consensus_Statement_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-2008_Consensus_Statement-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pediatrics.2C_Sports_Related_Concussion_in_Children_and_Adolescents.2C_2010_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-Pediatrics.2C_Sports_Related_Concussion_in_Children_and_Adolescents.2C_2010-2">[2]</a></sup> After this initial period has passed, there is debate as to whether it is necessary to awaken the person several times during the first night, as has traditionally been done, or whether there is more benefit from uninterrupted sleep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pediatrics.2C_Sports_Related_Concussion_in_Children_and_Adolescents.2C_2010_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-Pediatrics.2C_Sports_Related_Concussion_in_Children_and_Adolescents.2C_2010-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17215534_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17215534-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Physical and cognitive rest should be continued until all symptoms have resolved with most (80–90%) concussions resolving in seven to ten days, although the recovery time may be longer in children and adolescents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2008_Consensus_Statement_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-2008_Consensus_Statement-1">[1]</a></sup> Cognitive rest includes reducing activities which require concentration and attention such as school work, video games, and text messaging.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2008_Consensus_Statement_1-7"><a href="#cite_note-2008_Consensus_Statement-1">[1]</a></sup> It has been suggested that even leisure reading can commonly worsen symptoms in children and adolescents and proposals include time off from school and attending partial days. Since students may appear 'normal', continuing education of relevant school personnel may be needed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pediatrics.2C_Sports_Related_Concussion_in_Children_and_Adolescents.2C_2010_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-Pediatrics.2C_Sports_Related_Concussion_in_Children_and_Adolescents.2C_2010-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Those with concussion are generally prescribed rest,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Willer06_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Willer06-70">[70]</a></sup> including adequate nighttime sleep as well as daytime rest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cdcfacts_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-cdcfacts-46">[46]</a></sup> Rest includes both physical and cognitive rest until symptoms clear<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2008_Consensus_Statement_1-8"><a href="#cite_note-2008_Consensus_Statement-1">[1]</a></sup> and a gradual return to normal activities at a pace that does not cause symptoms to worsen is recommended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cdcfacts_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-cdcfacts-46">[46]</a></sup> Education about symptoms, their management, and their normal time course, can lead to an improved outcome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ComperBisschop_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-ComperBisschop-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>For persons participating in athletics, the 2008 Zurich Consensus Statement on Concussion in Sport recommends that participants be symptom-free before restarting and then progress through a series of graded steps. These steps include:</p>
<ul>
<li>complete physical and cognitive rest</li>
<li>light aerobic activity (less than 70% of maximum heart rate)</li>
<li>sport-specific activities such as running drills and skating drills</li>
<li>non-contact training drills (exercise, coordination, and cognitive load)</li>
<li>full-contact practice</li>
<li>full-contact games.</li>
</ul>
<p>Only when symptom-free for 24 hours, should progression to the next step occur. If symptoms occur, the person should drop back to the previous asymptomatic level for at least another 24 hours. The emphasis is on remaining symptom free and taking it in medium steps, not on the steps themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2008_Consensus_Statement_1-9"><a href="#cite_note-2008_Consensus_Statement-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Medications may be prescribed to treat sleep problems and depression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ComperBisschop_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-ComperBisschop-13">[13]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Analgesic" title="Analgesic">Analgesics</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Ibuprofen" title="Ibuprofen">ibuprofen</a> can be taken for headache,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AndersonT_9-7"><a href="#cite_note-AndersonT-9">[9]</a></sup> but <a href="/wiki/Paracetamol" title="Paracetamol">paracetamol (acetaminophen)</a> is preferred to minimize the risk of intracranial hemorrhage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Concussed individuals are advised not to use <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohol</a> or other <a href="/wiki/Drug" title="Drug">drugs</a> that have not been approved by a doctor as they can impede healing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Activation database-guided EEG biofeedback has been shown to return the memory abilities of the concussed individual to levels better than the control group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>About one percent of people who receive treatment for MTBI need surgery for a brain injury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BorgHolm04_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-BorgHolm04-48">[48]</a></sup> Observation to monitor for worsening condition is an important part of treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson03_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson03-74">[74]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Health_care" title="Health care">Health care</a> providers recommend that those suffering from concussion return for further medical care and evaluation 24 to 72 hours after the concussive event if the symptoms worsen. Athletes, especially intercollegiate or <a href="/wiki/Professional_sports" title="Professional sports">professional</a>, are typically followed closely by team athletic trainers during this period but others may not have access to this level of health care and may be sent home with minimal monitoring.</p>
<p>People may be released after assessment from hospital or emergency room to the care of a trusted person with instructions to return if they display worsening symptoms<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17215534_3-6"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17215534-3">[3]</a></sup> or those that might indicate an emergent condition such as: change in consciousness, convulsions, severe headache, extremity weakness, vomiting, new bleeding or deafness in either or both ears.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NICE1_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-NICE1-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prognosis">Prognosis</span></h2>
<p>People who have had a concussion seem more susceptible to another one, particularly if the new injury occurs before symptoms from the previous concussion have completely gone away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merck_Home_Health_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-Merck_Home_Health-11">[11]</a></sup> It is also a negative process if smaller impacts cause the same symptom severity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2008_Consensus_Statement_1-10"><a href="#cite_note-2008_Consensus_Statement-1">[1]</a></sup> Repeated concussions may increase a person's risk in later life for dementia, Parkinson's disease, and depression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merck_Home_Health_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-Merck_Home_Health-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>MTBI has a mortality rate of almost zero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BorgHolm04_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-BorgHolm04-48">[48]</a></sup> The symptoms of most concussions resolve within weeks, but problems may persist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AndersonT_9-8"><a href="#cite_note-AndersonT-9">[9]</a></sup> These are seldom permanent, and outcome is usually excellent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iverson2005_36-7"><a href="#cite_note-Iverson2005-36">[36]</a></sup> The overall prognosis for recovery may be influenced by a variety of factors that include age at the time of injury, intellectual abilities, family environment, social support system, occupational status, coping strategies, and financial circumstances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> People over age 55 may take longer to heal from MTBI or may heal incompletely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AlexanderMP95_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-AlexanderMP95-77">[77]</a></sup> Similarly, factors such as a previous head injury or a coexisting medical condition have been found to predict longer-lasting post-concussion symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hall05_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hall05-40">[40]</a></sup> Other factors that may lengthen recovery time after MTBI include psychological problems such as <a href="/wiki/Substance_abuse" title="Substance abuse">substance abuse</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clinical_depression" title="Clinical depression">clinical depression</a>, poor health before the injury or additional injuries sustained during it, and life stress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iverson2005_36-8"><a href="#cite_note-Iverson2005-36">[36]</a></sup> Longer periods of amnesia or loss of consciousness immediately after the injury may indicate longer recovery times from residual symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Masferrer_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Masferrer-78">[78]</a></sup> For unknown reasons, having had one concussion significantly increases a person's risk of having another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moser_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-moser-49">[49]</a></sup> Having previously sustained a sports concussion has been found to be a strong factor increasing the likelihood of a concussion in the future. Other strong factors include participation in a contact sport and body mass size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schulz04_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schulz04-79">[79]</a></sup> The prognosis may differ between concussed adults and children; little research has been done on concussion in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pediatric" title="Pediatric">pediatric</a> population, but concern exists that severe concussions could interfere with <a href="/wiki/Neural_development" title="Neural development">brain development</a> in children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moser_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-moser-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2009 study found that individuals with a history of concussions might demonstrate a decline in both physical and mental performance for longer than 30 years. Compared to their peers with no history of brain trauma, sufferers of concussion exhibited effects including loss of <a href="/wiki/Episodic_memory" title="Episodic memory">episodic memory</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bradykinesia" title="Bradykinesia">reduced muscle speed</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-De2009_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-De2009-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-concussion_syndrome">Post-concussion syndrome</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Post-concussion_syndrome" title="Post-concussion syndrome">Post-concussion syndrome</a></div>
<p>In post-concussion syndrome, symptoms do not resolve for weeks, months, or years after a concussion, and may occasionally be permanent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ryan03_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ryan03-81">[81]</a></sup> About 10% to 20% of people have post concussion syndrome for more than a month.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Symptoms may include headaches, dizziness, fatigue, <a href="/wiki/Anxiety" title="Anxiety">anxiety</a>, memory and attention problems, sleep problems, and irritability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boake_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boake-83">[83]</a></sup> There is no scientifically established treatment, and rest, a recommended recovery technique, has limited effectiveness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Willer06_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Willer06-70">[70]</a></sup> Symptoms usually go away on their own within months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ParikhKoch_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-ParikhKoch-66">[66]</a></sup> The question of whether the syndrome is due to structural damage or other factors such as psychological ones, or a combination of these, has long been the subject of debate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bryant08_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bryant08-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cumulative_effects">Cumulative effects</span></h3>
<p>Cumulative effects of concussions are poorly understood, with this being even more true in children. The severity of concussions and their symptoms may worsen with successive injuries, even if a subsequent injury occurs months or years after an initial one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harmon_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-harmon-84">[84]</a></sup> Symptoms may be more severe and changes in <a href="/wiki/Neurophysiology" title="Neurophysiology">neurophysiology</a> can occur with the third and subsequent concussions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moser_49-3"><a href="#cite_note-moser-49">[49]</a></sup> Studies have had conflicting findings on whether athletes have longer recovery times after repeat concussions and whether cumulative effects such as impairment in cognition and memory occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pellman_26-3"><a href="#cite_note-Pellman-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>Cumulative effects may include <a href="/wiki/Mental_disorder" title="Mental disorder">psychiatric disorders</a> and loss of <a href="/wiki/Long-term_memory" title="Long-term memory">long-term memory</a>. For example, the risk of developing clinical depression has been found to be significantly greater for retired <a href="/wiki/American_football" title="American football">American football</a> players with a history of three or more concussions than for those with no concussion history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cantuCTE_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-cantuCTE-85">[85]</a></sup> Three or more concussions is also associated with a fivefold greater chance of developing <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s_disease" title="Alzheimer's disease">Alzheimer's disease</a> earlier and a threefold greater chance of developing <a href="/wiki/Memory" title="Memory">memory</a> deficits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cantuCTE_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-cantuCTE-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="CTE">CTE</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chronic_traumatic_encephalopathy" title="Chronic traumatic encephalopathy">Chronic traumatic encephalopathy</a></div>
<p>Chronic traumatic encephalopathy, or "CTE", is an example of the cumulative damage that can occur as the result of multiple concussions or less severe blows to the head. The condition was previously referred to as "<a href="/wiki/Dementia_pugilistica" title="Dementia pugilistica">dementia pugilistica</a>", or "punch drunk" syndrome, as it was first noted in boxers. The disease can lead to cognitive and physical handicaps such as <a href="/wiki/Parkinsonism" title="Parkinsonism">parkinsonism</a>, speech and memory problems, slowed mental processing, tremor, depression, and inappropriate behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mendez_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mendez-86">[86]</a></sup> It shares features with Alzheimer's disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jordan00_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jordan00-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second-impact_syndrome">Second-impact syndrome</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Second-impact_syndrome" title="Second-impact syndrome">Second-impact syndrome</a></div>
<p>Second-impact syndrome, in which the brain swells dangerously after a minor blow, may occur in very rare cases. The condition may develop in people who receive a second blow days or weeks after an initial concussion, before its symptoms have gone away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowen03_15-5"><a href="#cite_note-Bowen03-15">[15]</a></sup> No one is certain of the cause of this often fatal complication, but it is commonly thought that the swelling occurs because the brain's <a href="/wiki/Arteriole" title="Arteriole">arterioles</a> lose the ability to regulate their diameter, causing a loss of control over cerebral blood flow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moser_49-4"><a href="#cite_note-moser-49">[49]</a></sup> As the brain <a href="/wiki/Swelling_(medical)" title="Swelling (medical)">swells</a>, intracranial pressure rapidly rises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CookSchweer_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-CookSchweer-44">[44]</a></sup> The brain can <a href="/wiki/Brain_herniation" title="Brain herniation">herniate</a>, and the brain stem can fail within five minutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowen03_15-6"><a href="#cite_note-Bowen03-15">[15]</a></sup> Except in boxing, all cases have occurred in athletes under age 20.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Team_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Team-37">[37]</a></sup> Due to the very small number of documented cases, the diagnosis is controversial, and doubt exists about its validity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCroryP01_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-McCroryP01-88">[88]</a></sup> A 2010 <i>Pediatrics</i> review article stated that there is debate whether the brain swelling is due to two separate hits or to just one hit, but in either case, catastrophic football head injuries are three times more likely in high school athletes than in college athletes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pediatrics.2C_Sports_Related_Concussion_in_Children_and_Adolescents.2C_2010_2-6"><a href="#cite_note-Pediatrics.2C_Sports_Related_Concussion_in_Children_and_Adolescents.2C_2010-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemiology">Epidemiology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MTBI_incidince_bar_graph.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="738" data-file-width="674" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/MTBI_incidince_bar_graph.svg/220px-MTBI_incidince_bar_graph.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/MTBI_incidince_bar_graph.svg/330px-MTBI_incidince_bar_graph.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/MTBI_incidince_bar_graph.svg/440px-MTBI_incidince_bar_graph.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MTBI_incidince_bar_graph.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Annual incidence of MTBI by age group in Canada<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gordonke_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-gordonke-89">[89]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Most cases of traumatic brain injury are concussions. A World Health Organization (WHO) study estimated that between 70 and 90% of head injuries that receive treatment are mild.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CassidyWHO_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-CassidyWHO-10">[10]</a></sup> However, due to underreporting and to the widely varying definitions of concussion and MTBI, it is difficult to estimate how common the condition is.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Petchprapai07_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-Petchprapai07-61">[61]</a></sup> Estimates of the incidence of concussion may be artificially low, for example due to underreporting. At least 25% of MTBI sufferers fail to get assessed by a medical professional.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iverson2005_36-9"><a href="#cite_note-Iverson2005-36">[36]</a></sup> The WHO group reviewed studies on the epidemiology of MTBI and found a hospital treatment rate of 1–3 per 1000 people, but since not all concussions are treated in hospitals, they estimated that the rate per year in the general population is over 6 per 1000 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CassidyWHO_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-CassidyWHO-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Young children have the highest concussion rate among all age groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17215534_3-7"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17215534-3">[3]</a></sup> However, most people who suffer concussion are young adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ryan03_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ryan03-81">[81]</a></sup> A Canadian study found that the yearly <a href="/wiki/Incidence_(epidemiology)" title="Incidence (epidemiology)">incidence</a> of MTBI is lower in older age groups (graph at right).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gordonke_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-gordonke-89">[89]</a></sup> Studies suggest males suffer MTBI at about twice the rate of their female counterparts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CassidyWHO_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-CassidyWHO-10">[10]</a></sup> However, female athletes may be at a higher risk for suffering concussion than their male counterparts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKeever03_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-McKeever03-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>Up to five percent of <a href="/wiki/Sports_injury" title="Sports injury">sports injuries</a> are concussions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Team_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-Team-37">[37]</a></sup> The U.S. <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">Centers for Disease Control and Prevention</a> estimates that 300,000 sports-related concussions occur yearly in the U.S., but that number includes only athletes who lost consciousness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Langlois06_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Langlois06-91">[91]</a></sup> Since loss of consciousness is thought to occur in less than 10% of concussions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cantu98_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cantu98-92">[92]</a></sup> the CDC estimate is likely lower than the real number.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Langlois06_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-Langlois06-91">[91]</a></sup> Sports in which concussion is particularly common include football and boxing (a boxer aims to "<a href="/wiki/Knockout" title="Knockout">knock out</a>", i.e. give a mild traumatic brain injury to, the opponent). The injury is so common in the latter that several medical groups have called for a ban on the sport, including the American Academy of Neurology, the <a href="/wiki/World_Medical_Association" title="World Medical Association">World Medical Association</a>, and the medical associations of the UK, the US, Australia, and Canada.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>Due to the lack of a consistent definition, the economic costs of MTBI are not known, but they are estimated to be very high.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BorgWHO_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-BorgWHO-94">[94]</a></sup> These high costs are due in part to the large percentage of hospital admissions for head injury that are due to mild head trauma,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LevinHS_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-LevinHS-63">[63]</a></sup> but indirect costs such as lost work time and early retirement account for the bulk of the costs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BorgWHO_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-BorgWHO-94">[94]</a></sup> These direct and indirect costs cause the expense of mild brain trauma to rival that of moderate and severe head injuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Silver05_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Silver05-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HippocraticOath.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="983" data-file-width="696" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/HippocraticOath.jpg/170px-HippocraticOath.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/HippocraticOath.jpg/255px-HippocraticOath.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/HippocraticOath.jpg/340px-HippocraticOath.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HippocraticOath.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Hippocratic_Corpus" title="Hippocratic Corpus">Hippocratic Corpus</a> mentioned concussion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Masferrer_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-Masferrer-78">[78]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Hippocratic Corpus, collection of medical works from ancient Greece, mentions concussion, later translated to <i>commotio cerebri</i>, and discusses loss of speech, hearing and sight that can result from "commotion of the brain".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Masferrer_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-Masferrer-78">[78]</a></sup> This idea of disruption of mental function by "shaking of the brain" remained the widely accepted understanding of concussion until the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Masferrer_78-3"><a href="#cite_note-Masferrer-78">[78]</a></sup> The Persian physician <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Zakar%C4%ABya_R%C4%81zi" title="Muhammad ibn Zakarīya Rāzi">Muhammad ibn Zakarīya Rāzi</a> was the first to write about concussion as distinct from other types of head injury in the 10th century AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCrory_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-McCrory-42">[42]</a></sup> He may have been the first to use the term "cerebral concussion", and his definition of the condition, a transient loss of function with no physical damage, set the stage for the medical understanding of the condition for centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivak_24-5"><a href="#cite_note-sivak-24">[24]</a></sup> In the 13th century, the physician <a href="/wiki/Lanfranc_of_Milan" title="Lanfranc of Milan">Lanfranc of Milan</a>'s <i>Chiurgia Magna</i> described concussion as brain "commotion", also recognizing a difference between concussion and other types of traumatic brain injury (though many of his contemporaries did not), and discussing the transience of post-concussion symptoms as a result of temporary loss of function from the injury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCrory_42-3"><a href="#cite_note-McCrory-42">[42]</a></sup> In the 14th century, the surgeon <a href="/wiki/Guy_de_Chauliac" title="Guy de Chauliac">Guy de Chauliac</a> pointed out the relatively good prognosis of concussion as compared to more severe types of head trauma such as <a href="/wiki/Skull_fracture" title="Skull fracture">skull fractures</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Penetrating_head_trauma" title="Penetrating head trauma">penetrating head trauma</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCrory_42-4"><a href="#cite_note-McCrory-42">[42]</a></sup> In the 16th century, the term "concussion" came into use, and symptoms such as confusion, lethargy, and memory problems were described.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCrory_42-5"><a href="#cite_note-McCrory-42">[42]</a></sup> The 16th century physician <a href="/wiki/Ambroise_Par%C3%A9" title="Ambroise Paré">Ambroise Paré</a> used the term <i>commotio cerebri</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivak_24-6"><a href="#cite_note-sivak-24">[24]</a></sup> as well as "shaking of the brain", "commotion", and "concussion".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Masferrer_78-4"><a href="#cite_note-Masferrer-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Guillaume_Dupuytren.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1308" data-file-width="1167" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Guillaume_Dupuytren.jpg/170px-Guillaume_Dupuytren.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Guillaume_Dupuytren.jpg/255px-Guillaume_Dupuytren.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Guillaume_Dupuytren.jpg/340px-Guillaume_Dupuytren.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Guillaume_Dupuytren.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Guillaume_Dupuytren" title="Guillaume Dupuytren">Guillaume Dupuytren</a> distinguished between concussion and unconsciousness associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brain_contusion" title="Brain contusion">brain contusion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Masferrer_78-5"><a href="#cite_note-Masferrer-78">[78]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Until the 17th century, concussion was usually described by its clinical features, but after the invention of the microscope, more physicians began exploring underlying physical and structural mechanisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCrory_42-6"><a href="#cite_note-McCrory-42">[42]</a></sup> However, the prevailing view in the 17th century was that the injury did not result from physical damage, and this view continued to be widely held throughout the 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCrory_42-7"><a href="#cite_note-McCrory-42">[42]</a></sup> The word "concussion" was used at the time to describe the state of unconsciousness and other functional problems that resulted from the impact, rather than a physiological condition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCrory_42-8"><a href="#cite_note-McCrory-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1839, Guillaume Dupuytren described brain contusions, which involve many small hemorrhages, as <i>contusio cerebri</i> and showed the difference between unconsciousness associated with damage to the brain <a href="/wiki/Parenchyma" title="Parenchyma">parenchyma</a> and that due to concussion, without such injury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Masferrer_78-6"><a href="#cite_note-Masferrer-78">[78]</a></sup> In 1941, animal experiments showed that no <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macroscopic" title="Macroscopic">macroscopic</a> damage occurs in concussion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Masferrer_78-7"><a href="#cite_note-Masferrer-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Denny-Brown_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Denny-Brown-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span></h3>
<p>The terms mild brain injury, mild traumatic brain injury (MTBI), mild head injury (MHI), and concussion may be used interchangeably;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ncipc_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-ncipc-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Petchprapai07_61-3"><a href="#cite_note-Petchprapai07-61">[61]</a></sup> although the term "concussion" is still used in sports literature as interchangeable with "MHI" or "MTBI", the general clinical medical literature uses "MTBI" instead, since a 2003 <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">CDC</a> report outlined it as an important strategy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BarthVarney99_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-BarthVarney99-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sivak_24-7"><a href="#cite_note-sivak-24">[24]</a></sup> In this article, "concussion" and "MTBI" are used interchangeably.</p>
<p>The term is from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>concutere</i> meaning "to shake violently"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pearce_30-3"><a href="#cite_note-Pearce-30">[30]</a></sup> or <i>concussus</i> meaning "action of striking together".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brooks_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brooks-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Minocycline" title="Minocycline">Minocycline</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lithium_(medication)" title="Lithium (medication)">lithium</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N-acetylcysteine" title="N-acetylcysteine">N-acetylcysteine</a> show tentative success in animal models.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Toledo_2012_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Toledo_2012-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>Measurement of predictive visual tracking is being studied as a screening technique to identify mild traumatic brain injury. A <a href="/wiki/Head-mounted_display" title="Head-mounted display">head-mounted display</a> unit with <a href="/wiki/Eye_tracking" title="Eye tracking">eye-tracking</a> capability shows a moving object in a predictive pattern for the person to follow with their eyes. People without brain injury will be able to track the moving object with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smooth_pursuit_eye_movements" title="Smooth pursuit eye movements">smooth pursuit eye movements</a> and correct <a href="/wiki/Trajectory" title="Trajectory">trajectory</a> while it is hypothesized that those with mild traumatic brain injury cannot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
